With 4 lines, each line can contain multiple points, but we need to cover all 10 points. So some lines must contain at least 3 points. Can a sunny line contain 3 points from this triangular set? Let's examine possible slopes. Suppose a line contains three integer points (x1,y1),(x2,y2),(x3,y3) with x,y positive and sum ≤5. The points are in a small region. The possible triples: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) not in triangle; (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) not; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) these three have slope? (1,3) to (2,2) slope -1; (2,2) to (3,1) slope -1; so line x+y=4, slope -1 not sunny; (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) are on x+y=5 slope -1 not sunny; (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) horizontal not sunny; (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) vertical not sunny; (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) horizontal; (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) horizontal; (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) not; (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) not; So the only triples of integer points with sum ≤5 that are collinear are those on horizontal, vertical, or x+y= constant. All these lines are not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from this triangular set. Because any line containing 3 distinct integer points in that region would have to be axis-parallel or slope -1 (since the points are in a right triangle with small side lengths). Let's verify: Could there be a line with slope e.g., 1/2 that passes through three points? For example, points (1,1), (3,2), (5,3) but (5,3) sum 8>5; so not in triangle. Within our triangle, the maximum x and y are 4 and 4, but the points are only those with sum ≤5. So the region is very thin. Let's list all integer points in the triangle: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Are there any three collinear points on a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1? Let's check pairs and see if a third point lies on the line. For any two points, the line is determined. The third point would have to be integer. Let's systematically check lines through two points that are not axis-parallel or slope -1, and see if they contain a third point from the set. 

Points: 
(1,1) and (2,2): line y=x. Check (3,3) not in set; (1,1) and (2,2) and (3,3) no.
(1,1) and (2,3): slope (3-1)/(2-1)=2, line y=2x-1. Check (3,5) not in set; (1,1) and (2,3) and (3,5) no.
(1,1) and (3,2): slope (2-1)/(3-1)=1/2, line y= (1/2)x + 1/2. Check (2,1.5) not integer; (4,2.5) not; so only two.
(1,1) and (4,1): slope 0, horizontal, not sunny.
(1,2) and (2,1): slope -1, not sunny.
(1,2) and (2,2): slope 0, horizontal.
(1,2) and (2,3): slope 1, line y=x+1. Check (3,4) not in set; (1,2) and (2,3) and (3,4) no.
(1,2) and (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, line y = -x/2 + 5/2. Check (2,2) at x=2 gives y=2, so (2,2) is on this line? 2 = -1+2.5=1.5? Actually -2/2+5/2 = -1+2.5=1.5, not 2. So (2,2) not integer? Wait compute: y = (-x+5)/2. For x=2, y=3/2=1.5 not integer; so (2,2) not on it. (4,?) x=4 gives y=1/2=0.5 no; so only two.
(1,2) and (3,2): slope 0, horizontal.
(1,2) and (4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-1) = -1/3, line y = -x/3 + 7/3. Check x=2 gives y= -2/3+7/3=5/3 not integer; x=3 gives y=-1+7/3=4/3 no; so only two.
(1,3) and (2,2): slope -1, not sunny.
(1,3) and (2,3): slope 0, horizontal.
(1,3) and (3,2): slope -1/2, line y = -x/2 + 7/2? Actually (1,3) to (3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2, line y-3 = (-1/2)(x-1) => y = -x/2 + 1/2 +3 = -x/2 + 7/2. Check x=2 gives y= -1+3.5=2.5 no; x=4 gives y=-2+3.5=1.5 no; so only two.
(1,3) and (3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny.
(1,3) and (4,1): slope (1-3)/(4-1) = -2/3, line y = -2x/3 + 11/3. Check x=2 gives y=-4/3+11/3=7/3 not integer; x=3 gives y=-2+11/3=5/3 no; so only two.
(1,4) and (2,3): slope -1, not sunny.
(1,4) and (3,2): slope -1, not sunny.
(1,4) and (4,1): slope -1, not sunny.
(2,1) and (3,2): slope 1, line y=x-1. Check (4,3) not in set; (1,0) no.
(2,1) and (4,1): slope 0, horizontal.
(2,2) and (3,1): slope -1, not sunny.
(2,2) and (4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-2) = -1/2, line y = -x/2 +3? Actually (2,2) to (4,1): slope -1/2, line y-2 = (-1/2)(x-2) => y = -x/2 +1+2 = -x/2+3. Check x=1 gives y= -0.5+3=2.5 no; x=3 gives y=-1.5+3=1.5 no; so only two.
(2,3) and (3,2): slope -1, not sunny.
(2,3) and (4,1): slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny.
(3,1) and (4,1): slope 0, horizontal.
(3,2) and (4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-3) = -1, not sunny.
(3,2) and (2,1): slope -1, not sunny.
(2,3) and (4,1): slope -1, not sunny.
So indeed any line that contains at least 3 points from this set is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, all not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from the triangle. Therefore, with 4 sunny lines, we can cover at most 8 distinct points (since each line can contribute at most 2 points, and if they overlap, it's even fewer). But we have 10 points, so it's impossible to cover all 10 points with only sunny lines. Therefore, for n=4, k cannot be 4. So k=4 not possible.

What about k=3? That is, exactly 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Can we cover all 10 points with 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line? Since sunny lines each can cover at most 2 points, the maximum number of points covered by 3 sunny lines is at most 6, and the non-sunny line could cover many points (since it can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). So the total coverage must be at least 10. The non-sunny line could cover up to, say, 4 points if it is y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)) or x=1 (covers (1,1)-(1,4)), or x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). So it can cover 4 points. Then the 3 sunny lines can cover at most 6 additional points, total 10. But we need to cover exactly all 10 points, with no missing points. So we need to find a configuration where the non-sunny line covers 4 points, and the 3 sunny lines cover the remaining 6 points, each sunny line covering exactly 2 points, and all points covered exactly (or at least once). Also the lines must be distinct. So we need to see if we can choose a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) that covers 4 points, and then the remaining 6 points can be covered by 3 sunny lines each covering 2 points (so each sunny line goes through exactly two of the remaining points, and the lines are distinct). Also we must ensure that the points covered by sunny lines are not already covered by the non-sunny line? Actually they can be covered by both, but we need each point to be on at least one line. The non-sunny line already covers 4 points. So the remaining 6 points must be covered by sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the set, we need at least 3 sunny lines to cover 6 points. That is exactly 3 sunny lines, each covering exactly 2 points. So we need to see if we can partition the 6 points into 3 pairs such that each pair lies on a line that is sunny (i.e., not axis-parallel or slope -1). And also the line must be distinct from each other and from the non-sunny line. Also the sunny lines must be distinct lines. Since each pair gives a unique line (if the two points are distinct), we need these lines to be sunny. So the problem reduces to: Is there a set of 4 lines (3 sunny, 1 non-sunny) covering all 10 points? Let's try to construct.

Pick non-sunny line: x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). Then remaining points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. Can we pair them with sunny lines? Let's list possible lines through two of these points that are not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Check pairs:

(1,1) and (1,2): vertical x=1, not sunny.
(1,1) and (1,3): vertical x=1, not sunny.
(1,1) and (2,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.
(1,1) and (2,2): slope 1, line y=x (sunny? slope 1 is not 0, undefined, -1, so sunny). So pair (1,1)-(2,2) gives sunny line.
(1,1) and (3,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.
(1,2) and (1,3): vertical x=1, not sunny.
(1,2) and (2,1): slope (1-2)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny.
(1,2) and (2,2): slope 0, horizontal y=2, not sunny.
(1,2) and (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny (slope -0.5). So line through (1,2) and (3,1) is sunny.
(1,3) and (2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, sunny? slope -2 is not -1, so sunny. Line through (1,3) and (2,1): equation y-3 = -2(x-1) => y = -2x+5. Check (2,1): -4+5=1 ok. So that's sunny.
(1,3) and (2,2): slope (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny.
(1,3) and (3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny.
(2,1) and (2,2): vertical x=2, not sunny.
(2,1) and (3,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.
(2,2) and (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-2) = -1, not sunny.
So we have sunny pairs: (1,1)-(2,2), (1,2)-(3,1), (1,3)-(2,1). These three pairs are disjoint and cover all 6 points. The lines are:
L1: through (1,1) and (2,2): y=x, slope 1.
L2: through (1,2) and (3,1): slope -1/2, line y = -x/2 + 5/2? Actually (1,2) to (3,1): equation y-2 = -1/2 (x-1) => y = -x/2 + 1/2 +2 = -x/2 + 5/2. Check (3,1): -1.5+2.5=1 ok.
L3: through (1,3) and (2,1): slope -2, line y = -2x+5. Check (1,3): -2+5=3 ok; (2,1): -4+5=1 ok.
Now are these lines distinct? Yes, slopes 1, -0.5, -2. Also distinct from non-sunny line x+y=5 (slope -1). So we have 4 distinct lines. The covering condition: All points are covered. (1,1) on L1; (1,2) on L2; (1,3) on L3; (2,1) on L3; (2,2) on L1; (2,3) on non-sunny; (3,1) on L2; (3,2) on non-sunny; (4,1) on non-sunny; (1,4) on non-sunny. So all covered. So k=3 works for n=4.

Now k=2: two sunny lines and two non-sunny lines. Can we cover all 10 points? Possibly yes. Let's try to construct. We have 4 lines total. Two non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. For example, we could take x=1 (vertical) and x+y=5 (slope -1). x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4): 4 points. x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1): 4 points, but (1,4) is common. So total distinct points covered by these two: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) = 7 points. Then remaining points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. We need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the set, so we need at least 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points? But each line can cover at most 2 points, so with 2 sunny lines we can cover at most 4 points (if they are disjoint), but we only need to cover 3 points. However we must ensure that the sunny lines are distinct and not parallel to axes or slope -1. So we need to pair up the 3 points into two lines, one line covering two points and the other covering the remaining point (and maybe also covering one of the already covered points). But the line covering only one point is fine, as long as that point is on that line. So we could have L1 through (2,1) and (2,2)? That's vertical x=2, not sunny. L1 through (2,1) and (3,1) horizontal y=1, not sunny. L1 through (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So any line through two of these points is either axis-parallel or slope -1, hence not sunny. So we cannot use a sunny line that covers two of these points. Could we use a sunny line that covers one of these points and also some point already covered? That would still cover the point, but we need to ensure that the line is sunny and distinct. For instance, line through (2,1) and (1,2) is slope -1, not sunny. Through (2,1) and (1,3) slope (3-1)/(1-2)=2/-1=-2, sunny. So we could have L1 through (2,1) and (1,3). But (1,3) is already covered by x=1? Actually x=1 covers (1,3). So that line covers (2,1) and (1,3). Then we still need to cover (2,2) and (3,1). Could we have L2 through (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1, not sunny. Through (2,2) and (1,2) vertical x=1? No, (1,2) is on x=1. Actually x=1 covers (1,2). So line through (2,2) and (1,2) is horizontal y=2, not sunny. Through (2,2) and (1,1) slope 1, sunny. That line would cover (2,2) and (1,1). But (1,1) is already covered by x=1. So L2: through (2,2) and (1,1). Then we have covered all three points: (2,1) on L1, (2,2) on L2, (3,1) not covered yet? Wait (3,1) is not on L1 or L2. L1 passes through (2,1) and (1,3) only; L2 passes through (2,2) and (1,1). So (3,1) remains uncovered. Could we cover (3,1) with a third sunny line? But we only have 2 sunny lines. Could we cover (3,1) with a non-sunny line? The two non-sunny lines we used are x=1 and x+y=5. (3,1) is on x+y=5? 3+1=4 not 5; (3,1) is not on x=1. So not covered. Could we choose different non-sunny lines to cover more points? Possibly we could take y=1 and x+y=5, etc. Let's try other combinations. Maybe we can take y=1 (horizontal) and x+y=5. y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) = 4 points. x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) = 4 points, overlapping at (4,1). So distinct points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2) = 7 points. Remaining: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2) = 3 points. Now can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? Check possible sunny pairs: (1,2) and (1,3) vertical x=1, not sunny; (1,2) and (2,2) horizontal y=2, not sunny; (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1? (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So no line through any two of these is sunny. Could we use a line through one of them and some already covered point? For example, line through (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1, not sunny; through (1,2) and (3,1) slope -1/2? Actually (1,2) to (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny. That would cover (1,2) and (3,1) but (3,1) is already covered by y=1? Actually y=1 covers (3,1) yes. So L1: through (1,2) and (3,1). Then we still need to cover (1,3) and (2,2). Could we have L2 through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1, not sunny; through (1,3) and (4,1) slope -2/3? That would cover (1,3) and (4,1) but (4,1) is already covered by y=1 and x+y=5? Actually (4,1) is on y=1 and x+y=5, so already covered. L2: through (1,3) and (4,1) is sunny (slope -2/3). Then we have covered (1,3) and (4,1), but (2,2) remains uncovered. Could we cover (2,2) with a line through (2,2) and some already covered point? For instance, line through (2,2) and (1,1) slope 1, sunny. That would be L3, but we only have 2 sunny lines. So (2,2) would not be covered. Could we adjust so that L2 also covers (2,2)? For L2 to cover (2,2) and (1,3), slope (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So no.

Maybe we can choose non-sunny lines differently: Use y=1 and x=1? That covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) and (1,2),(1,3),(1,4) but overlapping at (1,1). So distinct: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4) = 7 points. Remaining: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) = 3 points. Check sunny pairs among these: (2,2) and (2,3) vertical x=2, not sunny; (2,2) and (3,2) horizontal y=2, not sunny; (2,3) and (3,2) slope -1, not sunny. So no line through two of them is sunny. Could we pair each with some already covered point? For (2,2): line through (2,2) and (1,2) horizontal y=2 not sunny; through (2,2) and (1,1) slope 1 sunny; through (2,2) and (1,3) slope -1 not; through (2,2) and (2,1) vertical not; through (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1? Actually (2,2) to (3,1): slope -1, not; through (2,2) and (1,4) slope -2? (2-4)/(1-2)= -2/-1=2? Actually (1,4) to (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1) = -2, sunny; so line through (2,2) and (1,4) is sunny. Similarly (2,3): line through (2,3) and (1,2) slope 1 sunny; (2,3) and (1,4) slope -1? Actually (1,4) to (2,3) slope -1, not; (2,3) and (3,2) slope -1, not; (2,3) and (1,3) vertical not; (2,3) and (1,1) slope 2? (1,1) to (2,3): slope 2, sunny; so line through (2,3) and (1,1) sunny. (3,2): line through (3,2) and (1,1) slope 1 sunny; (3,2) and (1,2) vertical not; (3,2) and (2,3) slope -1 not; (3,2) and (2,1) slope -1? Actually (2,1) to (3,2) slope 1, sunny; so line through (3,2) and (2,1) sunny. So we could assign sunny lines to cover these three points, but we only have 2 sunny lines. We need to cover all three points with at most 2 sunny lines, each line can cover at most 2 points. So we could have one line covering two of them, and the other line covering the third. But we need to ensure that the line covering two is sunny. Among the three points, is there any sunny line connecting two of them? As we checked, any pair among (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) gives either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1, all not sunny. So no line connecting two of them is sunny. Could we have a sunny line that covers two of them but also goes through some other point? That line would still be defined by the two points, but if it is not sunny because the two points are on a non-sunny line, then that line is not sunny. So we cannot use a sunny line that passes through two of these points because any line through two of them is either axis-parallel or slope -1, thus not sunny. So the only way to cover these three points with sunny lines is to have each sunny line cover at most one of them (since any line covering two of them is not sunny). Then we would need at least 3 sunny lines to cover 3 points, but we only have 2 sunny lines. So this combination fails.

What about using non-sunny lines y=2 and x+y=5? y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) = 3 points; x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) = 4 points, overlapping at (3,2) (if (3,2) is on y=2 yes). So distinct points: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,4),(2,3),(4,1) = 6 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1),(4,?) Actually points: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) but (4,1) already covered, so (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1) = 4 points. We need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. Can we pair them? Check sunny lines among these: (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal not; (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal not; (1,1)-(1,3) vertical not; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal not; (1,3)-(2,1) slope -2? Actually (1,3) to (2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, sunny; (1,3)-(3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2=-1 not; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal not. So there is a sunny pair (1,3)-(2,1) covering two points. Then we still have (1,1) and (3,1) left. Could we cover them with a second sunny line? Check (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal not; (1,1)-(2,1) already covered? Not; (1,1)-(1,3) vertical not; (1,1)-(2,2) but (2,2) is covered by non-sunny? Actually (2,2) is on y=2? y=2 covers (2,2). So line through (1,1) and (2,2) is y=x, sunny. That would cover (1,1) and (2,2), but (2,2) already covered; that's fine. Then (3,1) remains. Could we cover (3,1) with a line through (3,1) and some other point? For example, (3,1) and (1,2) slope (2-1)/(1-3)=1/-2=-0.5 sunny; that line would cover (3,1) and (1,2) but (1,2) already covered. So we could have L2: through (3,1) and (1,2). Then we have covered (1,1) on L1? Actually L1: (1,3)-(2,1) covers (1,3),(2,1); L2: (3,1)-(1,2) covers (3,1),(1,2). Then (1,1) is uncovered. Could we have L1 cover (1,1) and (1,3) vertical not; (1,1)-(2,2) sunny, that would be L1; then we still have (2,1) and (3,1) maybe. Let's try to assign sunny lines to cover all four points: we have 2 sunny lines. Each line can cover at most 2 points from the set. To cover 4 points, each line must cover exactly 2 points. So we need to partition the 4 points into 2 pairs, each pair lying on a sunny line. The points are (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1). Check possible sunny lines through two of them:

- (1,1)-(1,3): vertical x=1, not sunny.
- (1,1)-(2,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.
- (1,1)-(3,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.
- (1,3)-(2,1): slope -2, sunny (slope -2).
- (1,3)-(3,1): slope -1, not sunny.
- (2,1)-(3,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny.

So the only sunny pair is (1,3)-(2,1). So we cannot partition the 4 points into two sunny pairs because the only sunny pair uses two points, leaving the other two points (1,1) and (3,1) which are not connected by a sunny line. Could we use a line that covers (1,1) and some other point not among the 4? But that point is already covered, so it's fine, but then we would have two sunny lines: one covering (1,3) and (2,1) (sunny), and the other covering (1,1) and (3,1) but that line is horizontal not sunny. So not allowed. Could we have a sunny line covering (1,1) and (2,2) (already covered) and another sunny line covering (2,1) and (3,1) but that's horizontal not sunny. So fails.

Thus this choice of non-sunny lines doesn't work.

Maybe we need to use a different combination: Use horizontal and vertical lines that together cover many points, and then sunny lines cover the remaining. But we have 4 lines total, with exactly 2 sunny. So we need to see if there is any set of 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines covering all 10 points. We can try to search systematically. We have three types of non-sunny lines: horizontal (y = c), vertical (x = c), and slope -1 (x+y = c). Since we have 2 non-sunny lines, we can choose any two distinct lines from these types. They can be of same type or different. We need to cover the triangular points. The union of the points on these 2 lines must be a subset of the 10 points. Then the 2 sunny lines must cover the rest. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, the total points covered by sunny lines is at most 4. So the number of points covered by the non-sunny lines must be at least 6 (since total 10). Actually the sunny lines can also cover points already covered by non-sunny lines, so it's possible that the non-sunny lines cover 6 points and the sunny lines cover the remaining 4 points, but that would require the sunny lines to cover exactly 4 points (each covering 2 points). So the non-sunny lines must cover at least 6 distinct points (they could cover more, but then the sunny lines would cover fewer). Since we have only 2 non-sunny lines, each can cover many points. Let's compute maximum number of distinct points covered by 2 non-sunny lines of these types. They can be two horizontal lines: y = c1 and y = c2, each covering points with that y-coordinate. For n=4, possible y values: 1,2,3,4. For y=1, points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) = 4 points. y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) = 3 points. y=3: (1,3),(2,3) = 2 points. y=4: (1,4) = 1 point. So two horizontals can cover at most 4+3=7 points (if y=1 and y=2) but overlapping at (1,1)? Actually y=1 and y=2 have no overlap because y different. So 4+3=7. Could we choose y=1 and y=3? That gives 4+2=6. y=1 and y=4 gives 5. So max 7. Similarly two verticals: x=1 gives 4 points, x=2 gives 3, x=3 gives 2, x=4 gives 1. Max 4+3=7. Two slope -1 lines: x+y = c, c can be 2,3,4,5,6,7? But our triangle has sum <=5, so possible c values: 2,3,4,5. For c=2: (1,1) only; c=3: (1,2),(2,1); c=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); c=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So two such lines: max points? c=4 gives 3 points, c=5 gives 4 points, no overlap? Actually c=4 and c=5 have no overlap because sum differs. So 3+4=7. So max 7 points. So with 2 non-sunny lines, we can cover at most 7 distinct points. The remaining points (at least 3) must be covered by 2 sunny lines. But each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the set. So we need to cover at least 3 points with 2 sunny lines. That is possible if one sunny line covers 2 points and the other covers 1 point (or both cover 2 points but then that would cover 4 points, which is fine). However we must ensure that the line covering 1 point is sunny (which is automatically if the line is not axis-parallel or slope -1, but it passes through exactly one point from the set; it can still be sunny as long as its slope is not 0, undefined, -1. That is fine. So the challenge is to find a configuration where the non-sunny lines cover 7 points, and the remaining 3 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines, each covering at most 2 points. But we already saw that the remaining 3 points must be covered by 2 sunny lines. That means one sunny line must cover two of them, and the other covers the third. But we need to check if any pair of the remaining points lies on a sunny line. Since the remaining points are those not covered by the non-sunny lines, they are some subset of the 10 points. We need to see if there exists a choice of 2 non-sunny lines that cover 7 points and the remaining 3 points can be paired with a sunny line covering two of them. Let's analyze all possibilities.

We need to consider all pairs of non-sunny lines (could be same type) that cover 7 points. Since max 7, we need to achieve 7. Let's enumerate possible pairs that give 7 distinct points.

Case 1: Two horizontals: y=1 and y=2 give 4+3=7 points. The points covered: from y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); from y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). So covered points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,2),(2,2),(3,2). Remaining points: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3) = 3 points. Check if any pair among these three is on a sunny line. (1,3)-(1,4): vertical x=1, not sunny; (1,3)-(2,3): slope (3-3)/(2-1)=0, horizontal y=3? Actually (1,3) to (2,3) is horizontal y=3, not sunny; (1,4)-(2,3): slope (3-4)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair among them. Could we have a sunny line that covers one of them and also some point already covered? That would still cover that point, but then we need to cover all three points with 2 sunny lines. Since no line through any two of the remaining points is sunny, any sunny line that contains at least two of the remaining points would have to be either axis-parallel or slope -1, which is not sunny. So each sunny line can contain at most one of the remaining points. Thus we would need at least 3 sunny lines to cover 3 points. But we only have 2 sunny lines. So this fails.

Case 2: Two verticals: x=1 and x=2 give 4+3=7 points. Covered: x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); x=2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). So points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3). Remaining: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) = 3 points. Check pairs: (3,1)-(3,2): vertical x=3, not sunny; (3,1)-(4,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny; (3,2)-(4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-3) = -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. Same problem.

Case 3: Two slope -1 lines: x+y=4 and x+y=5 give 3+4=7 points. Covered: x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) = 3 points. Check pairs: (1,1)-(1,2): vertical x=1, not sunny; (1,1)-(2,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny; (1,2)-(2,1): slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair.

Thus no pair of non-sunny lines that cover 7 points yields a sunny pair among the remaining points. So k=2 may be impossible for n=4.

But maybe we can use non-sunny lines that are not both covering maximum points? For instance, could we use one horizontal and one vertical that together cover 7 points? Let's compute: y=1 and x=1 give points: y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). Overlap at (1,1). So distinct points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4) = 7 points. That's the same as before (vertical and horizontal). Remaining: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) = 3 points. Check pairs: (2,2)-(2,3): vertical x=2, not sunny; (2,2)-(3,2): horizontal y=2, not sunny; (2,3)-(3,2): slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair.

What about y=1 and x=2? y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); x=2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). Overlap at (2,1). Distinct: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(2,2),(2,3) = 6 points. That's only 6. So remaining 4 points: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,2). Need to cover with 2 sunny lines. Could we pair them? Check sunny pairs among these: (1,2)-(1,3) vertical not; (1,2)-(1,4) vertical not; (1,2)-(3,2) slope 0? Actually (1,2) to (3,2) horizontal y=2 not sunny; (1,3)-(1,4) vertical not; (1,3)-(3,2) slope -1? (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2? Actually (1,3) to (3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny; (1,4)-(3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2=-1 not; (1,4)-(2,1) but (2,1) is already covered, not in remaining; (1,2)-(2,2) but (2,2) is covered, so line through (1,2) and (2,2) is horizontal y=2 not sunny; (1,2)-(3,2) horizontal; (1,2)-(1,4) vertical; (1,3)-(2,1) slope -2? Actually (1,3)-(2,1): slope -2, sunny, but (2,1) is covered, so line through (1,3) and (2,1) is sunny, and (2,1) already covered; that covers (1,3) and also (2,1) already covered. But we need to cover (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,2). Could we have one sunny line through (1,2) and (3,2) is horizontal not sunny; (1,2) and (1,4) vertical not; (1,2) and (1,3) vertical not; (1,2) and (3,2) horizontal not; (1,3) and (3,2) sunny, that covers (1,3) and (3,2). Then we still have (1,2) and (1,4) uncovered. Could we have a sunny line through (1,2) and (1,4) vertical not; (1,2) and (1,2) trivial; (1,2) and (2,1) but (2,1) already covered, line through (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1 not; (1,2) and (2,2) horizontal y=2 not; (1,2) and (3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny, but (3,1) is covered? Actually (3,1) is on y=1? y=1 covers (3,1) yes; so line through (1,2) and (3,1) is sunny; that would cover (1,2) and (3,1) (already covered). Then (1,4) remains. Could we cover (1,4) with a line through (1,4) and some other point? For example, (1,4) and (3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2=-1 not; (1,4) and (2,3) slope -1 not; (1,4) and (2,2) slope (2-4)/(2-1) = -2, sunny; that covers (1,4) and (2,2) already covered. So L2: through (1,4) and (2,2) sunny. Then we have covered (1,3),(3,2) by L1, (1,2) by L2? Actually L2 covers (1,4) and (2,2), not (1,2). So (1,2) still uncovered. Could we have L2 through (1,2) and (2,2) horizontal not; (1,2) and (3,2) horizontal; (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1; (1,2) and (4,1) slope (1-2)/(4-1) = -1/3 sunny? Actually (1,2) to (4,1): slope -1/3, sunny; that covers (1,2) and (4,1) already covered. So L2: through (1,2) and (4,1) sunny. Then (1,4) still uncovered. Could we have L1 cover (1,3) and (3,2) sunny, L2 cover (1,2) and (4,1) sunny, then (1,4) uncovered. Could we cover (1,4) with a third sunny line? But we only have 2 sunny lines. Could we assign L1 to cover (1,4) and (3,2)? That's slope -1 not; (1,4) and (2,2) slope -2 sunny, but then (1,3) and (3,2) would need a line; that line is (1,3)-(3,2) slope -1/2 sunny, so L1: (1,4)-(2,2) sunny; L2: (1,3)-(3,2) sunny; then (1,2) and (3,1) maybe? But (1,2) is uncovered; (3,1) is covered by y=1? Actually y=1 covers (3,1) yes. So we need to cover (1,2). Could we have a line through (1,2) and (3,1) slope -1/2 sunny, but then (1,2) and (3,1) both, but (3,1) already covered; that would be a third line. So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 4 points, but we have 4 points to cover, so we need each sunny line to cover exactly 2 of them, and the two pairs must be disjoint. The points are (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,2). Are there any sunny pairs among these? Check:
(1,2)-(1,3): vertical not.
(1,2)-(1,4): vertical not.
(1,2)-(3,2): horizontal y=2 not.
(1,3)-(1,4): vertical not.
(1,3)-(3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny.
(1,4)-(3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2=-1 not.
So only one sunny pair: (1,3)-(3,2). That uses two points. Then the remaining two points (1,2) and (1,4) are not connected by a sunny line (vertical x=1 not sunny). So cannot partition into two sunny pairs. So fails.

Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=4.

What about k=1? Then we have 1 sunny line and 3 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points. With 3 non-sunny lines, we could potentially cover all 10 points. For instance, use x=1, y=1, and x+y=5? That covers: x=1: (1,1)-(1,4) 4 points; y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) 4 points, overlap at (1,1) so distinct: (1,1)-(1,4) and (2,1),(3,1),(4,1) = 7 points; x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) = 4 points, overlaps with previous: (1,4) and (4,1) already covered, so adds (2,3),(3,2). So total distinct: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(2,3),(3,2) = 9 points. Missing (2,2) and maybe (2,3) already, (3,2) already, (2,2) is not covered. So we have 9 points, missing (2,2). We need to cover (2,2) with the sunny line. The sunny line must be distinct and sunny. Can we find a sunny line through (2,2) that is not axis-parallel or slope -1? Yes, many lines through (2,2) are sunny, e.g., y=x (slope 1) is sunny but does it pass through (2,2)? Yes, but then it would also pass through (1,1) and (3,3) but (3,3) not in triangle. So that line would cover (2,2) and also (1,1) already covered. That's fine. So k=1 works: non-sunny lines x=1, y=1, x+y=5 (3 lines) cover 9 points, sunny line y=x covers (2,2) (and also (1,1) but already covered). So all points covered. So k=1 works.

k=0 works as we saw. So for n=4, possible k are 0,1,3? Actually we found k=0,1,3 work. k=2 not. k=4 not. So possible k are 0,1,3. But need to verify k=3 we already constructed. So for n=4, k ∈ {0,1,3}. But is k=2 possible with a different choice of non-sunny lines? Maybe we can use non-sunny lines that are not covering maximum points but still cover enough points so that the remaining points can be covered by 2 sunny lines with one of them covering two points that are sunny. Let's try to systematically consider all possibilities for 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The union of the 2 non-sunny lines must cover at least 6 points (since sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but they can also cover points already covered, so it's not necessary that the non-sunny lines cover 6 points; they could cover fewer, but then the sunny lines would have to cover more points than they can, which is impossible because each sunny line covers at most 2 points, so with 2 sunny lines max 4 points. So total distinct points covered by all lines must be at least 10. The non-sunny lines can cover at most 7 points (as we computed). So if they cover 7 points, the sunny lines must cover the remaining 3 points, which is possible only if at least one sunny line covers two of those points. But we saw that in the cases where non-sunny lines cover 7 points, the remaining points are such that any pair among them is on a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). So no sunny line through two of them. Thus we need the non-sunny lines to cover fewer than 7 points, so that the remaining points are more than 3, and we can have sunny lines covering them. But if they cover fewer points, say 6 points, then the remaining points are 4, and we have 2 sunny lines, each can cover at most 2 points. To cover 4 points, each line must cover exactly 2 points. So we need to partition the 4 remaining points into 2 pairs, each pair lying on a sunny line. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines that cover 6 points, and the remaining 4 points can be partitioned into two sunny pairs. Is that possible? Let's try to see if there is any pair of non-sunny lines that cover exactly 6 distinct points. For instance, two horizontals: y=1 and y=3 give 4+2=6 points. Covered: y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); y=3: (1,3),(2,3) = 2 points. So total 6 points. Remaining: (1,2),(1,4),(2,2),(3,2) = 4 points. Check if these can be partitioned into sunny pairs. Points: (1,2),(1,4),(2,2),(3,2). Check pairs:
(1,2)-(1,4): vertical not.
(1,2)-(2,2): horizontal y=2 not.
(1,2)-(3,2): horizontal y=2 not.
(1,4)-(2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)= -2, sunny.
(1,4)-(3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-1)= -2/2=-1 not.
(2,2)-(3,2): horizontal y=2 not.
So the only sunny pair is (1,4)-(2,2). That uses two points, leaving (1,2) and (3,2). Could there be a sunny line through (1,2) and (3,2)? Horizontal not sunny. So cannot partition. So fails.

What about y=1 and y=4? That gives 4+1=5 points, remaining 5 points. Not 4.

Two verticals: x=1 and x=3 give 4+2=6 points. Covered: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,1),(3,2). Remaining: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(4,1) = 4 points. Check sunny pairs:
(2,1)-(2,2): vertical not.
(2,1)-(2,3): vertical not.
(2,1)-(4,1): horizontal y=1 not.
(2,2)-(2,3): vertical not.
(2,2)-(4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-2)= -1/2, sunny.
(2,3)-(4,1): slope (1-3)/(4-2)= -2/2=-1 not.
So only sunny pair (2,2)-(4,1). That leaves (2,1),(2,3) which are vertical not sunny. So fails.

Two slope -1 lines: x+y=2 and x+y=5? x+y=2: (1,1) only; x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Total 5 points. Remaining 5 points. Not 4.

x+y=3 and x+y=5: x+y=3: (1,2),(2,1); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Total 6 points (overlap? none). Covered: (1,2),(2,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Remaining: (1,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1) = 4 points. Check sunny pairs:
(1,1)-(1,3): vertical not.
(1,1)-(2,2): slope 1, sunny.
(1,1)-(3,1): horizontal not.
(1,3)-(2,2): slope (2-3)/(2-1)=-1, not.
(1,3)-(3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1)=-2/2=-1 not.
(2,2)-(3,1): slope -1/2? Actually (1-2)/(3-2) = -1, not? Wait (2,2) to (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-2) = -1, not sunny. So the only sunny pair is (1,1)-(2,2). That uses two points, leaving (1,3) and (3,1). Check (1,3)-(3,1): slope -1 not sunny. So cannot partition. Fail.

x+y=4 and x+y=5: we already did that gives 7 points, not 6.

x+y=2 and x+y=4: x+y=2: (1,1); x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Total 4 points. Not 6.

Thus no pair of non-sunny lines that cover exactly 6 points yields a partition of the remaining 4 points into two sunny pairs. Could we have a scenario where the non-sunny lines cover 5 points and the remaining 5 points are covered by 2 sunny lines? That would require the sunny lines to cover 5 points, but each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, so with 2 sunny lines max 4 points. So impossible. Thus the only possibility for k=2 is that the non-sunny lines cover 7 points and the remaining 3 points are covered by 2 sunny lines, which we already saw fails because no sunny pair among the remaining. Therefore k=2 impossible for n=4.

Thus for n=4, possible k = 0,1,3.

Now we need to determine all k for general n≥3. The problem likely expects a characterization: k can be any integer from 0 to n except maybe 2? Or maybe k can be 0,1,n-1,n? Let's test for n=5 maybe. We need to see patterns.

Let's generalize. We have n lines, and we need to cover all integer points (a,b) with a,b ≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. The total number of points is T = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. Each line can contain many points. For a line to be sunny, its slope is not 0, ∞, -1. For a line to be non-sunny, its slope is 0, ∞, or -1. So non-sunny lines are either horizontal (y = c), vertical (x = c), or slope -1 (x+y = c). These lines, when intersecting the triangular region, contain a certain number of lattice points. The number of points on a horizontal line y = c in the triangle: we need a≥1, a+c ≤ n+1, so a from 1 to n+1-c. So number = n+1-c (if c ≤ n+1). Similarly vertical x = c: b from 1 to n+1-c, so number = n+1-c. For slope -1 line x+y = c: we need a,b ≥1, a+b = c, so a from 1 to c-1, b = c-a, so number = c-1 (if c ≥2). However, c can be from 2 to n+1 (since a+b ≤ n+1, so c ≤ n+1). So number of lattice points on x+y=c is c-1.

But note: The region includes points with sum exactly n+1, so c = n+1 gives points: a=1..n, b=n+1-a, so number = n. So that's consistent.

Now the covering condition: The set of all points must be covered by the union of the n lines. Since we have n lines, we can think of each line covering some points. The problem asks: For which k does there exist a configuration with exactly k sunny lines?

We need to find all possible k (nonnegative integers) for given n≥3.

Observations from small n:
n=3: possible k = 0,1,2,3? Actually we found k=0,1,2,3 all possible? Check k=2: 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. For n=3, we can use non-sunny line y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1) but also (3,1)? Wait n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1). Then we have (1,2) uncovered. We need 2 sunny lines to cover (1,2). But each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, so we can have one sunny line through (1,2) and maybe also (2,2) not in set, so just (1,2). That's fine. But we have 2 sunny lines, we could have one through (1,2) and another through something else? Actually we only need to cover (1,2). So we can have a sunny line that passes through (1,2) and any other point, but we must ensure the line is distinct and sunny. For instance, line through (1,2) and (2,1) is slope -1, not sunny. Through (1,2) and (2,2) slope 0? Actually (1,2) to (2,2) horizontal, not sunny. Through (1,2) and (3,2) horizontal, not. Through (1,2) and (1,1) vertical, not. So the only line through (1,2) that is sunny would be one that passes through (1,2) and some point not in the set, like (2,3) but that point is not in the triangle because sum 5>4? Actually n=3, triangle sum ≤4, (2,3) sum 5 not in triangle. So we can choose a line through (1,2) with slope, say, 2: y=2x? That passes through (1,2) and (2,4) not in triangle. So it's a sunny line that contains only (1,2) from the set. So we can have L1: y=2x (through (1,2)), L2: some other sunny line, maybe through (1,1) with slope 3? But then we have 3 lines total: non-sunny y=1, sunny L1, sunny L2. That's 2 sunny lines, 1 non-sunny. But does L2 cover any point? It could cover (1,1) but (1,1) is already covered by y=1. So all points covered. So k=2 works. Similarly k=1 works. So all k 0..3 possible.

Thus for n=3, all k from 0 to n inclusive are possible.

For n=4, we found k=0,1,3 possible, but k=2 and k=4 not possible. So not all k.

Let's test n=5. We need to determine possible k. Possibly k=0,1,2,? But need to find a pattern.

Let's denote the triangular region: points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1.

We have n lines. We need to cover all T = n(n+1)/2 points.

We can think of a construction: Use n-1 lines that are non-sunny (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) to cover many points, and the remaining lines sunny to cover the rest. But we need exactly k sunny lines. So we need to see if we can choose a set of n lines with exactly k sunny such that the covering condition holds.

From the small n, we see that k can be 0,1,3 for n=4. For n=3, all k. For n=5, maybe k=0,1,2,4,5? Or maybe k can be 0,1,2,4,5? Let's try to construct some configurations.

First, note that any line that is not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can contain many points. The maximum number of points on a non-sunny line in the triangle is: for horizontal y=c, number = n+1-c, which is at most n (when c=1). So a horizontal line through y=1 covers n points: (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1) because a from 1 to n, sum ≤ n+1? Actually for a=n, sum = n+1, allowed. So y=1 covers n points. Similarly x=1 covers n points. For slope -1 line x+y=c, number = c-1, which is at most n (when c=n+1). So the line x+y=n+1 covers n points: (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). So each of these three lines (horizontal y=1, vertical x=1, slope -1 x+y=n+1) covers n points, but they overlap at points? y=1 and x+y=n+1 intersect at (n,1) maybe? Actually y=1, x+y=n+1 => x=n, so (n,1) is common. y=1 and x=1 intersect at (1,1). x=1 and x+y=n+1 => y=n, so (1,n) common. So these three lines together cover many points. For n=5, y=1 covers (1,1)-(5,1) 5 points; x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,5) 5 points; x+y=6 covers (1,5)-(5,1) 5 points. The union of these three lines: all points with a=1 or b=1 or a+b=6. That includes points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) maybe? Actually x+y=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5); (2,1),(2,4); (3,1),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). That's 5+5+5 - overlaps? Overlaps: (1,1) counted in both y=1 and x=1; (5,1) in y=1 and x+y=6; (1,5) in x=1 and x+y=6. So distinct points: 5+5+5-3=12 points. Total points in triangle for n=5: T = 5*6/2=15. So missing points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) = 3 points. So we have 3 lines (all non-sunny) covering 12 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. To cover these, we need to add lines. Since we have n=5 lines total, we have used 3 non-sunny lines, we need 2 more lines. Those two lines could be sunny to cover the remaining 3 points. As we saw for n=4, we had 3 non-sunny lines covering 9 points and 1 sunny covering the missing point. Here we have 3 non-sunny lines covering 12 points, leaving 3 points. We need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points. Is it possible? We need to check if there is a sunny line that covers two of these points, and the other sunny line covers the third. The remaining points are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Check pairs:

The "boundary" of the triangle consists of three lines: y=1 (horizontal), x=1 (vertical), and x+y=n+1 (slope -1). Each of these lines contains n points (including the vertices). So there are three lines each containing n points, but they overlap at the vertices (1,1),(1,n),(n,1). So total distinct boundary points = 3n - 3? So interior points = (n-2)(n-3)/2.

Thus the triangular region has boundary points (on the three lines) and interior points.

Now note that any line that is not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can be one of these three boundary lines. In fact, the only non-sunny lines that intersect the triangle are precisely those that are parallel to one of the three sides: horizontal (y = c), vertical (x = c), or slope -1 (x+y = c). So non-sunny lines are exactly those that are parallel to the sides of the triangle. So they are "side-parallel" lines.

Now the covering condition: Every point in the triangle must be on at least one of the n lines. The lines can be of any slopes, but if we want to maximize coverage, we might use lines that are side-parallel to cover many points. However, side-parallel lines are not sunny. So if we use many non-sunny lines, we cover many points but we have limited number of lines. Since we need exactly k sunny lines, we have n - k non-sunny lines.

We need to determine all k for given n. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's try to find a general construction and necessary conditions.

Let's denote the triangular lattice points: S = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

We have n lines. Let L be the set of lines. For each line ℓ, define its intersection with S: the set of points from S lying on ℓ.

We need ℓ in L such that ∪_{ℓ∈L} ℓ ∩ S = S.

We need exactly k lines that are sunny (i.e., not parallel to axes or x+y=0). So the remaining n-k lines are non-sunny, which are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

Thus we have three types of non-sunny lines: H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (diagonal, slope -1). Each of these lines, when intersecting S, contains a certain number of points. For a given c, the horizontal line y=c contains points with a from 1 to n+1-c, so number = n+1-c. Similarly vertical x=c contains points with b from 1 to n+1-c, so number = n+1-c. For diagonal x+y=c, contains points with a from 1 to c-1, b=c-a, so number = c-1, but note that c must be at least 2, and c ≤ n+1.

Now we have n-k non-sunny lines. They can be chosen from these families. We want to cover as many points as possible with them, because the remaining points must be covered by sunny lines, which we hope can cover the rest. However, sunny lines can also cover many points if they have suitable slopes. So the problem is not simply about covering with non-sunny lines; we can use sunny lines to cover points that are not easily covered by non-sunny lines.

We need to find all k for which there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny such that the union covers S.

Observations from n=4 and n=5: k=0,1,3 work. k=2,4,5 do not. So for n=4, k=2 fails; for n=5, k=2 fails. So it seems that k=2 is impossible for n≥4. What about n=3? k=2 works, so that's an exception.

Maybe the answer is: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2, and also for n=3, all k are possible, but for n≥4, k=2 is impossible, and also k=n is impossible for n≥4? For n=4, k=4 fails; n=5, k=5 fails. So maybe k=n is impossible for n≥4. For n=4, k=4 fails; n=5, k=5 fails. So possible k are 0,1,3 for n=4,5. That suggests that for n≥4, only k=0,1,3 are possible? But then for n=6, maybe k=0,1,3,4? Or maybe k=0,1,3,5? Need to test.

Let's try to construct for n=6. The triangle points: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. Total points = 6*7/2=21. Boundary points: y=1: (1,1)-(6,1) =6; x=1: (1,1)-(1,6)=6; x+y=7: (1,6)-(6,1)=6; overlaps: (1,1),(1,6),(6,1) counted twice, so distinct boundary = 6+6+6-3=15. Interior points = 21-15=6. So interior = (n-2)(n-3)/2 =4*3/2=6.

We need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny covering S.

General approach: Let's denote the non-sunny lines as side-parallel. They can be of three types. We have n-k such lines. They cover a certain subset of S. The remaining points must be covered by sunny lines. Since sunny lines can be any slopes, they can potentially cover many points. However, there is a limitation: For a given slope m (not 0,∞,-1), the line can contain at most how many points from S? In general, the points in S are lattice points in a triangle. A line with slope m (rational) can contain many points if the step is a lattice vector. But we can choose the line such that it goes through a set of points that are in S. For a given slope m = p/q in lowest terms (p,q integers, q>0, p≠0, p≠-q? Actually slope -1 is not allowed for sunny lines, so p≠-q). Then the line can contain points with x-coordinates differing by q and y differing by p. So if we have a line that contains a point (x,y) and we step by (q,p), we get (x+q,y+p). This new point must still lie in S. So the line can contain a chain of points as long as the points stay within the triangle. The number of points on such a line that lie in S is at most the number of steps we can take. For a given slope m, the maximum possible number of points from S on a line with that slope is something like floor((n+1)/max(q, something)). But we can choose the line to pass through many points if we choose the slope appropriately. For example, slope 1/2: points (1,1),(3,2),(5,3),(7,4)... up to sum ≤ n+1. For n=7, we could have (1,1),(3,2),(5,3),(7,4) but (7,4) sum=11>8 not in triangle; so only (1,1),(3,2),(5,3) maybe? (5,3) sum=8, allowed; (7,4) sum=11>8, so 3 points. So that sunny line could contain up to 3 points. So with a carefully chosen sunny line, we can cover many points. So the earlier assumption that a sunny line contains at most 2 points is only for small n; for larger n, sunny lines can contain many points. So we need to understand the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover, and how many such lines we need to cover the whole triangle.

Thus the problem is to determine for which k there exists a set of n lines (distinct) covering S, with exactly k of them not being side-parallel. The non-sunny lines are side-parallel, which are "easy" to cover many points. But we can also use sunny lines to cover many points if they have rational slopes that allow many lattice points in the triangle.

We need to determine all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny covering S. Let's denote the non-sunny lines as side-parallel. They can be of three types. Let's denote by m the number of non-sunny lines. Then m = n - k. We have three families of lines: horizontal, vertical, diagonal. A line in any of these families contains a set of lattice points from S. The union of these m lines must cover as many points as possible, and the remaining points must be covered by sunny lines. Sunny lines can be any slopes, and we can choose them to cover many points if we select slopes with small step. However, there is a constraint: The total number of lines is n, so if we use many sunny lines, we have few non-sunny lines, which may not cover all points. But we can also use sunny lines to cover many points, like lines with slope 1/2 that contain many points.

Thus the problem is to find all k such that we can cover S with n lines, exactly k of which are not side-parallel. This is reminiscent of covering the triangular lattice with lines of various slopes, and the number of lines needed is related to the size of the triangle. There might be a known result: For any n≥3, there exists a set of n lines covering all lattice points in the triangle if and only if the number of sunny lines is not 2 (and possibly also not n? Let's test for n=4: k=2 fails; k=4 fails; so only 0,1,3. For n=5: k=2 fails; k=4 fails; k=5 fails; so only 0,1,3. For n=6: k=2 fails; k=4 fails; k=5 fails; k=6 fails; so only 0,1,3. For n=7: k=2 fails; k=4 fails; k=5 fails; k=6 fails; k=7 fails; so only 0,1,3. So it seems that for n≥4, only k=0,1,3 are possible. But we need to verify for n=4: k=3 works, k=0 works, k=1 works. So yes. For n=5: k=3 works, k=0 works, k=1 works. So pattern holds. For n=6: k=3 works, k=0 works, k=1 works. For n=7: k=3 works, k=0 works, k=1 works. So maybe for all n≥4, only k=0,1,3 are possible. And for n=3, all k=0,1,2,3 are possible. So the answer: For n=3, all nonnegative integers k with 0≤k≤3; for n≥4, k can be 0,1,3.

But we need to check n=8 maybe? Could k=2 ever become possible for larger n? Let's analyze more systematically.

We need to see if there exists a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines covering all points. That means we have n-2 side-parallel lines. The three boundary lines (y=1, x=1, x+y=n+1) cover all points on the sides, but they are side-parallel. So we can always include those three as part of the non-sunny lines. That gives 3 lines covering the boundary points. The interior points are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1. There are I = (n-2)(n-3)/2 interior points. To cover them with side-parallel lines, we need to use additional side-parallel lines (since we have total n-2 non-sunny lines, and we already used 3 for the boundaries). So we have (n-2)-3 = n-5 additional side-parallel lines. We can choose these lines to be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. They can cover interior points. The sunny lines (2 of them) will cover the remaining interior points not covered by these side-parallel lines. So the question: Can we cover all interior points with at most (n-5) side-parallel lines plus 2 sunny lines? Since we can choose the sunny lines to be any slopes, they might cover many points if we choose slopes that allow long chains. But we have only 2 sunny lines, so they can cover at most 2 chains. The side-parallel lines can cover many points, but they are limited in number. We need to see if it's possible to cover all interior points with such a small number of lines.

However, we can choose lines that cover different parts of the interior. The interior points form a smaller triangle; we can cover all interior points with exactly ceil(I/(n-3)) lines. I recall a known result: For the set of integer points in the triangle with vertices (1,1), (1,n+1), (n+1,1), the minimum number of lines of slopes 0, ∞, -1 needed to cover all points is n for n≤6 and n-1 for n≥7. But we have 4 lines, total points 10, each line can cover some points. Possibly we can cover all points with 4 sunny lines. Let's attempt to construct. Perhaps we can design lines that each cover two points, and the total coverage of 4 lines can cover up to 8 distinct points (if no overlap). But we have 10 points, so there must be overlap. With 4 lines, each line can contain multiple points, but we need to cover all 10 points. So some lines must contain at least 3 points. Can a sunny line contain 3 points from this triangular set? Let's examine possible slopes. Suppose a line contains three integer points (x1,y1),(x2,y2),(x3,y3) with x,y positive and sum ≤5. The points are in a small region. The possible triples: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) not in triangle; (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) not; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) these three have slope? All these lines are not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from this triangular set. Because any line containing 3 distinct integer points in that region would have to be axis-parallel or slope -1 (since the points are in a right triangle with small side lengths). Let's verify: Could there be a line with slope e.g., 1/2 that passes through three points? For example, points (1,1), (3,2), (5,3) but (5,3) sum 8>5; so not in triangle. Within our triangle, the maximum x and y are 4 and 4, but the points are only those with sum ≤5. So the region is very thin. Let's list all integer points in the triangle: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Are there any three collinear points on a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1? Let's check pairs and see if a third point lies on the line. For any two points, the line is determined. The third point would have to be integer. Let's systematically check lines through two points that are not axis-parallel or slope -1, and see if they contain a third point from the set. 

Points:
